4570 <section id="_260">

4571

4572 <h2>

4573Treatment of Appendiceal Neuroendocrine Tumors</h2>

4574

4575 <div id="_section_260" class="pdq-sections">

4576<nav class="in-this-section" role="navigation">

4577<h6>

4578In This Section</h6>

4579<ul>

4580<li>

4581<a href="#_TrialSearch_260_sid_8_toc">

4582Current Clinical Trials</a>

4583</li>

4584</ul>

4585</nav>

4586<p id="_261" tabindex="-1">

4587Approximately 90% of appendiceal neuroendocrine (carcinoid) tumors measure smaller than 1 cm and are not located in the appendiceal base. These tumors can be consistently cured by appendectomy.[<a href="#cit/section_8.1">

45881</a>

4589]</p>

4590<p id="_262" tabindex="-1">

4591Appendiceal neuroendocrine tumors larger than 2 cm require right-sided hemicolectomy and ileocecal lymphadenectomy because of the significant risk of metastasis.[<a href="#cit/section_8.1">

45921</a>

4593] For tumors measuring 1 to 2 cm, treatment is controversial, but hemicolectomy may be appropriate if there is invasion in the mesoappendix, if there is residual tumor in the resection margins, or in the presence of lymph node metastases. For same-size lesions confined to the appendiceal wall, appendectomy alone may carry a low risk for metastases. Acceptable indications for hemicolectomy may include operative specimens that show high proliferative activity (high Ki-67 index), high mitotic index, or signs of angioinvasion, but evidence is limited and histological parameters for risk evaluation in appendiceal neuroendocrine tumors measuring 1 cm to 2 cm requires definition.[<a href="#cit/section_8.1">

45941</a>

4595-<a href="#cit/section_8.3">

45963</a>

4597] Follow-up should be considered in patients for whom elevated serum chromogranin A may indicate the need for extended operation. Although survival is excellent with locoregional tumor, 10-year survival is approximately 30% with distant metastases.[<a href="#cit/section_8.1">

45981</a>

4599]</p>

4600<p id="_263" tabindex="-1">

4601Goblet cell carcinoid or adenocarcinoid is a rare variant of appendiceal neuroendocrine tumor with mixed endocrine and exocrine features.[<a href="#cit/section_8.1">

46021</a>

4603] Often presenting with a diffusely inflamed appendix and occurring in patients at a later age (~50 years), these tumors are aggressive, often with peritoneal and ovarian metastases, and occasionally appearing as mucinous adenocarcinoma.[<a href="#cit/section_8.2">

46042</a>

4605-<a href="#cit/section_8.4">

46064</a>

4607] They do not express somatostatin receptors and cannot be visualized by indium In 111-octreotide scintigraphy. Goblet cell carcinoids are treated with right-sided hemicolectomy and lymphadenectomy in combination with chemotherapy. For disseminated tumors, aggressive surgical reduction including peritonectomy and oophorectomy may be required.[<a href="#cit/section_8.1">

46081</a>

4609] Goblet cell carcinoids have a 10-year survival rate of approximately 60%.[<a href="#cit/section_8.2">

46102</a>

4611]</p>

4646 <section id="_264">

4647

4648 <h2>

4649Treatment of Colonic Neuroendocrine Tumors</h2>

4650

4651 <div id="_section_264" class="pdq-sections">

4652<nav class="in-this-section" role="navigation">

4653<h6>

4654In This Section</h6>

4655<ul>

4656<li>

4657<a href="#_TrialSearch_264_sid_9_toc">

4658Current Clinical Trials</a>

4659</li>

4660</ul>

4661</nav>

4662<p id="_265" tabindex="-1">

4663Colonic neuroendocrine (carcinoid) tumors are often exophytic and large (&gt;5 cm), but they rarely bleed. Only occasional right-sided lesions are positive with indium In 111-octreotide scintigraphy. Many of these tumors are aggressive with a high proliferation rate, and they often present with more liver metastases than regional lymph node metastases.[<a href="#cit/section_9.1">

46641</a>

4665] These tumors of the colon are treated similarly to adenocarcinoma of the colon.[<a href="#cit/section_9.2">

46662</a>

4667] Attempts to achieve radical resection by hemicolectomy or subtotal colectomy with lymphadenectomy should be made, but frequently only debulking is possible. The overall 5-year survival rate is approximately 40% and is slightly worse than the survival rate for patients with colon adenocarcinoma.[<a href="#cit/section_9.1">

46681</a>

4669]</p>

4688Pl&#246;ckinger U, Rindi G, Arnold R, et al.: Guidelines for the diagnosis and treatment of neuroendocrine gastrointestinal tumours. A consensus statement on behalf of the European Neuroendocrine Tumour Society (ENETS). Neuroendocrinology 80 (6): 394-424, 2004.&#160;<a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&amp;db=PubMed&amp;list_uids=15838182&amp;dopt=Abstract" title="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&amp;db=PubMed&amp;list_uids=15838182&amp;dopt=Abstract">

4689[PUBMED Abstract]</a>

4690</li>

4691</ol>

4692</div>

4693

4694 </section>

4695

4696

4697

4698 <section id="_266">

4699

4700 <h2>

4701Treatment of Rectal Neuroendocrine Tumors</h2>

4702

4703 <div id="_section_266" class="pdq-sections">

4704<nav class="in-this-section" role="navigation">

4705<h6>

4706In This Section</h6>

4707<ul>

4708<li>

4709<a href="#_TrialSearch_266_sid_10_toc">

4710Current Clinical Trials</a>

4711</li>

4712</ul>

4713</nav>

4714<p id="_315" tabindex="-1">

4715In general, rectal neuroendocrine (carcinoid) tumors often present as very small, isolated lesions.[<a href="#cit/section_10.1">

47161</a>

4717] The TNM (tumor, node, metastasis) system is used for rectal neuroendocrine tumors, but size appears to be one of the best estimates of recurrence. Rectal neuroendocrine tumors should be evaluated by endoscopic ultrasonography (EUS) or rectal magnetic resonance imaging (MRI). Tumors smaller than 1 cm can be safely removed by endoscopic excision.[<a href="#cit/section_10.2">

47182</a>

4719-<a href="#cit/section_10.5">

47205</a>

4721] Excised specimens should be examined histologically to exclude muscularis invasion.[<a href="#cit/section_10.2">

47222</a>

4723,<a href="#cit/section_10.6">

47246</a>

4725-<a href="#cit/section_10.8">

47268</a>

4727]. A report about the patients with rectal carcinoid tumors in the Surveillance, Epidemiology, and End Results (SEER) Program database demonstrated that the 5-year survival rate for patients with stage I carcinoid tumors was 97%.[<a href="#cit/section_10.9">

47289</a>

4729]</p>

4730<p id="_316" tabindex="-1">

4731For patients with tumors that are larger than 2 cm or that have invasion of the muscularis as seen by EUS or MRI, surgical resection with abdominoperineal resection (APR) or low anterior resection (LAR) is recommended because of the high rate of nodal metastases and risk of distant metastatic disease. In the report from the SEER database, patients with stage II or III rectal carcinoid tumors had 5-year survival rates of 84% and 20%, respectively.[<a href="#cit/section_10.9">

47329</a>

4733] In a report from the National Cancer Database, among 3,287 patients with rectal carcinoid tumors, the 5-year survival rates for patients with stage II or III disease were 87.3% and 35.5%, respectively.[<a href="#cit/section_10.10">

473410</a>

4735] </p>

4736<p id="_317" tabindex="-1">

4737There is considerable debate about whether local excision or rectal resection (i.e., APR or LAR) is needed for tumors that measure 1 cm to 2 cm. Although it may be possible to recognize tumors with particular atypia and high mitotic index before embarking on the more radical surgery, the presence of muscularis invasion or regional metastases generally supports rectal resection. In a multicenter series of 100 patients who underwent anterior resection for rectal carcinoid tumors, the rate of nodal metastases for patients with tumors between 1 cm and 2 cm was 31%.[<a href="#cit/section_10.11">

473811</a>

4739] In this series, tumor size larger than 1 cm and lymphovascular invasion were the two strongest predictors of lymph node metastases. In patients with distant metastases, prognosis is generally poor, with an overall 5-year survival rate of approximately 30%.[<a href="#cit/section_10.12">

474012</a>

4741]</p>

4742<section id="_TrialSearch_266_sid_10">

4743<h3 id="_TrialSearch_266_sid_10_toc">

4747advanced clinical trial search</a>

4748 to find NCI-supported cancer clinical trials that are now enrolling patients. The search can be narrowed by location of the trial, type of treatment, name of the drug, and other criteria. <a href="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials/" title="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials/">

4749General information</a>

4750 about clinical trials is also available.</p>

4808 <section id="_270">

4809

4810 <h2>

4811Treatment of Metastatic Gastrointestinal Neuroendocrine Tumors</h2>

4812

4813 <div id="_section_270" class="pdq-sections">

4814<nav class="in-this-section" role="navigation">

4815<h6>

4816In This Section</h6>

4817<ul>

4818<li>

4819<a href="#_TrialSearch_270_sid_11_toc">

4820Current Clinical Trials</a>

4821</li>

4822</ul>

4823</nav>

4824<p id="_271" tabindex="-1">

4825Although the definitive role of surgery in patients with metastatic disease has not been established, conservative resections of the intestine, mesenteric tumors, and fibrotic areas may improve symptoms and quality of life substantially in patients with metastatic hepatic, mesenteric, and peritoneal neuroendocrine (carcinoid) tumors. If the condition of the patient is such that surgery is not a greater risk than the disease, the primary tumor should be resected to prevent an emergency presentation with obstruction, perforation, or bleeding.[<a href="#cit/section_11.1">

48261</a>

4827] Despite common acceptance that resection of at least 90% of the tumor burden is required to achieve palliation, approximately 60% of patients with surgery alone will experience symptom recurrence; the 5-year survival rate is between 35% and 80%, depending on the experience of the surgical center.[<a href="#cit/section_11.2">

48282</a>

4829,<a href="#cit/section_11.3">

48303</a>

4831] Because treatment with somatostatin analogues can achieve similar rates of symptom relief with fewer adverse effects, in each patient the benefits of surgical treatment of gastrointestinal neuroendocrine tumors should be weighed carefully against the potential risks of an open exploration. Tumor debulking, however, may potentiate pharmacological therapy by decreasing the secretion of bioactive substances.[<a href="#cit/section_11.4">

48324</a>

4833]</p>

4834<p id="_272" tabindex="-1">

4835Management of hepatic metastases may include surgical resection; hepatic artery embolization; cryoablation and radiofrequency ablation; and orthotopic liver transplantation. For more information, see the <a href="/types/gi-neuroendocrine-tumors/hp/gi-neuroendocrine-treatment-pdq#_93">

4836Treatment of Hepatic Metastases</a>

4837 section. Cytoreductive surgery for hepatic metastases from gastrointestinal neuroendocrine tumors can be performed safely with minimal morbidity and mortality resulting in regression of symptoms and prolonged survival in most patients.[<a href="#cit/section_11.5">

48385</a>

4839] In one large review that included 120 patients with neuroendocrine tumors, a biochemical response rate of 96% and a 5-year survival rate of 61% were reported for patients whose hepatic metastases were resected surgically.[<a href="#cit/section_11.6">

48406</a>

4841][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810037&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810037&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

4842Level of evidence C2</a>

4843]</p>

4844<p id="_273" tabindex="-1">

4845In the case of liver metastases, localization and resection of the primary tumor may be considered, even among patients in whom the primary neoplasm is asymptomatic. In a retrospective study involving 84 patients, 60 of whom had their primary neoplasm resected, the resected group had a greater median progression-free survival (PFS) of 56 months, compared with 25 months of PFS for the primary nonresected group (<em>

4846P</em>

4847 &lt; .001). Median survival time for the resected group was longer at 159 months when compared with 47 months for the nonresected group (<em>

4848P</em>

4849 &lt; .001).[<a href="#cit/section_11.7">

48507</a>

4851][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810037&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810037&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

4852Level of evidence C2</a>

4853]</p>

4854<p id="_274" tabindex="-1">

4855Although the response of neuroendocrine tumors to external-beam radiation therapy is very limited, palliative radiation therapy has some efficacy for bone and brain metastases and in the management of spinal cord metastases.[<a href="#cit/section_11.4">

48564</a>

4857]</p>

4858<p id="_275" tabindex="-1">

4859Treatment with single-agent chemotherapy or multiple-agent chemotherapy appears to be of little benefit in the management of gastrointestinal neuroendocrine tumors because no regimen has shown objective tumor response rates greater than 15%.[<a href="#cit/section_11.4">

48604</a>

4861]</p>

4862<p id="_276" tabindex="-1">

4863Treatment with radionuclides such as iodine I 131-metaiodobenzylguanidine and lutetium Lu 177-octreotate may be of benefit. For more information, see the <a href="/types/gi-neuroendocrine-tumors/hp/gi-neuroendocrine-treatment-pdq#_91">

4864Radionuclides</a>

4865 section.</p>

4866<p id="_277" tabindex="-1">

4867Somatostatin analogues and interferon-alpha are the primary agents used in the treatment of carcinoid syndrome. Management of the symptoms of carcinoid syndrome may also include dietary modification and the use of various antidiarrheal agents, antihistaminics for skin rashes, and theophylline or beta-2 adrenergic receptor agonists for bronchospasm. For more information, see the sections on <a href="/types/gi-neuroendocrine-tumors/hp/gi-neuroendocrine-treatment-pdq#_79">

4868Somatostatin Analogues</a>

4869, <a href="/types/gi-neuroendocrine-tumors/hp/gi-neuroendocrine-treatment-pdq#_85">

4870Interferons</a>

4871, and <a href="/types/gi-neuroendocrine-tumors/hp/gi-neuroendocrine-treatment-pdq#_97">

4872Symptomatic Therapy</a>

4873. </p>

4874<p id="_278" tabindex="-1">

4875Information about ongoing clinical trials is available from the <a href="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials" title="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials">

4876NCI website</a>

4877.</p>

4926 <section id="_279">

4927

4928 <h2>

4929Treatment of Recurrent Gastrointestinal Neuroendocrine Tumors</h2>

4930

4931 <div id="_section_279" class="pdq-sections">

4932<nav class="in-this-section" role="navigation">

4933<h6>

4934In This Section</h6>

4935<ul>

4936<li>

4937<a href="#_TrialSearch_279_sid_12_toc">

4938Current Clinical Trials</a>

4939</li>

4940</ul>

4941</nav>

4942<p id="_280" tabindex="-1">

4943The prognosis for any patient with progressive or recurrent disease is poor. Decisions about further treatment depend on many factors, including previous treatment, site of recurrence, and individual patient considerations. Attempts at re-resecting slow-growing tumors (e.g., repeat or multiple liver resections) are worthy of consideration after extensive evaluation, as further reduction of tumor volume may provide long-term palliation. Recurrence at any single site may also be potentially resectable. Clinical trials are appropriate and should be considered when possible. </p>

4944<p id="_281" tabindex="-1">

4945Information about ongoing clinical trials is available from the <a href="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials" title="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials">

4946NCI website</a>

4947.</p>

4954 to find NCI-supported cancer clinical trials that are now enrolling patients. 4982the literature and does not represent a policy statement of NCI or NIH. More

4984maintaining the PDQ summaries can be found on the <a href="/types/gi-neuroendocrine-tumors/hp/gi-neuroendocrine-treatment-pdq#_AboutThis_1">

4985About This PDQ Summary</a>

4986 and <a href="https://www.cancer.gov/publications/pdq" title="https://www.cancer.gov/publications/pdq">

4987PDQ&#174; Cancer Information for Health Professionals</a>

4988 pages.

4989</p>

4990</div>

4991

4992 </section>

4993

4994

4995

4996 <section id="_AboutThis_1">

4997

4998 <h2>

4999About This PDQ Summary</h2>

5000

5001 <div id="_section_AboutThis_1" class="pdq-sections">

5002<section id="_AboutThis_2">

5003<h3 id="_AboutThis_2_toc">

5004Purpose of This Summary</h3>

5005<p id="_AboutThis_3" tabindex="-1">

5006This PDQ cancer information summary for health professionals provides comprehensive, peer-reviewed, evidence-based information about the treatment of adult gastrointestinal neuroendocrine tumors. It is intended as a resource to inform and assist clinicians in the care of their patients. It does not provide formal guidelines or recommendations for making health care decisions.</p>

5007</section>

5008<section id="_AboutThis_4">

5009<h3 id="_AboutThis_4_toc">

5010Reviewers and Updates</h3>

5011<p id="_AboutThis_5" tabindex="-1">

5012This summary is reviewed regularly and updated as necessary by the <a href="https://www.cancer.gov/publications/pdq/editorial-boards/adult-treatment" title="https://www.cancer.gov/publications/pdq/editorial-boards/adult-treatment">

5013PDQ Adult Treatment Editorial Board</a>

5014, which is editorially independent of the National Cancer Institute (NCI). The summary reflects an independent review of the literature and does not represent a policy statement of NCI or the National Institutes of Health (NIH).</p>

5015<p id="_AboutThis_22" tabindex="-1">

5016 Board members review recently published articles each month to determine whether an article should:</p>

5017<div class="pdq-content-list">

5018<ul id="_AboutThis_6">

5019<li>

5020be discussed at a meeting,</li>

5021<li>

5022be cited with text, or</li>

5023<li>

5024replace or update an existing article that is already cited.</li>

5025</ul>

5026</div>

5027<p id="_AboutThis_7" tabindex="-1">

5028Changes to the summaries are made through a consensus process in which Board members evaluate the strength of the evidence in the published articles and determine how the article should be included in the summary.</p>

5029<p id="" tabindex="-1">

5030The lead reviewers for Gastrointestinal Neuroendocrine Tumors Treatment are:</p>

5031<div class="pdq-content-list">

5032<ul id="">

5033<li>

5034Amit Chowdhry, MD, PhD (University of Rochester Medical Center)</li>

5035<li>

5036Arjun Gupta, MD (University of Minnesota Health Cancer Care)</li>

5037<li>

5038Leon Pappas, MD, PhD (Dana-Farber Cancer Institute)</li>

5039</ul>

5040</div>

5041<p id="_AboutThis_9" tabindex="-1">

5042Any comments or questions about the summary content should be submitted to Cancer.gov through the NCI website's <a href="https://www.cancer.gov/contact/email-us" title="https://www.cancer.gov/contact/email-us">

5043Email Us</a>

5044. Do not contact the individual Board Members with questions or comments about the summaries. Board members will not respond to individual inquiries.</p>

5045</section>

5046<section id="_AboutThis_10">

5047<h3 id="_AboutThis_10_toc">

5048Levels of Evidence</h3>

5049<p id="_AboutThis_11" tabindex="-1">

5050Some of the reference citations in this summary are accompanied by a level-of-evidence designation. These designations are intended to help readers assess the strength of the evidence supporting the use of specific interventions or approaches. The PDQ Adult Treatment Editorial Board uses a <a href="/publications/pdq/levels-evidence/treatment">

5051formal evidence ranking system</a>

5052 in developing its level-of-evidence designations.</p>

5053</section>

5054<section id="_AboutThis_12">

5055<h3 id="_AboutThis_12_toc">

5056Permission to Use This Summary</h3>

5057<p id="_AboutThis_13" tabindex="-1">

5058PDQ is a registered trademark. Although the content of PDQ documents can be used freely as text, it cannot be identified as an NCI PDQ cancer information summary unless it is presented in its entirety and is regularly updated. However, an author would be permitted to write a sentence such as &#8220;NCI&#8217;s PDQ cancer information summary about breast cancer prevention states the risks succinctly: [include excerpt from the summary].&#8221;</p>

5059<p id="_AboutThis_14" tabindex="-1">

5060The preferred citation for this PDQ summary is:</p>

5061<p id="_AboutThis_15" tabindex="-1">

5062PDQ&#174; Adult Treatment Editorial Board. PDQ Gastrointestinal Neuroendocrine Tumors Treatment. Bethesda, MD: National Cancer Institute. Updated &lt;MM/DD/YYYY&gt;. Available at: <a href="https://www.cancer.gov/types/gi-neuroendocrine-tumors/hp/gi-neuroendocrine-treatment-pdq" title="https://www.cancer.gov/types/gi-neuroendocrine-tumors/hp/gi-neuroendocrine-treatment-pdq">

5063https://www.cancer.gov/types/gi-neuroendocrine-tumors/hp/gi-neuroendocrine-treatment-pdq</a>

5064. Accessed &lt;MM/DD/YYYY&gt;. [PMID: 26389233]</p>

5065<p id="_AboutThis_16" tabindex="-1">

5066Images in this summary are used with permission of the author(s), artist, and/or publisher for use within the PDQ summaries only. Permission to use images outside the context of PDQ information must be obtained from the owner(s) and cannot be granted by the National Cancer Institute. Information about using the illustrations in this summary, along with many other cancer-related images, is available in <a href="https://visualsonline.cancer.gov/" title="https://visualsonline.cancer.gov/">

5067Visuals Online</a>

5068, a collection of over 2,000 scientific images.

5069

5070</p>

5071</section>

5072<section id="_AboutThis_17">

5073<h3 id="_AboutThis_17_toc">

5074Disclaimer</h3>

5075<p id="_AboutThis_18" tabindex="-1">

5076Based on the strength of the available evidence, treatment options may be described as either &#8220;standard&#8221; or &#8220;under clinical evaluation.&#8221; These classifications should not be used as a basis for insurance reimbursement determinations. More information on insurance coverage is available on Cancer.gov on the <a href="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/managing-care" title="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/managing-care">

5077Managing Cancer Care</a>

5078 page.</p>

5079</section>
